<p id="mwBg"><span typeof="mw:Nowiki mw:Transclusion" about="#mwt5" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"pp-semi-indef","href":"./Template:Pp-semi-indef"},"params":{},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBw"></span><meta typeof="mw:Extension/indicator" about="#mwt5" data-mw='{"name":"indicator","attrs":{"name":"pp-default"},"body":{"extsrc":"[[File:Semi-protection-shackle.svg|20px|link=Wikipedia:Protection policy#semi|alt=Page semi-protected|This article is semi-protected.]]"},"html":"&lt;span typeof=\"mw:File\" data-parsoid=&apos;{\"optList\":[{\"ck\":\"width\",\"ak\":\"20px\"},{\"ck\":\"link\",\"ak\":\"link=Wikipedia:Protection policy#semi\"},{\"ck\":\"alt\",\"ak\":\"alt=Page semi-protected\"},{\"ck\":\"caption\",\"ak\":\"This article is semi-protected.\"}]}&apos; data-mw=&apos;{\"caption\":\"This article is semi-protected.\"}&apos;>&lt;a href=\"./Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi\" title=\"This article is semi-protected.\" data-parsoid=\"{}\">&lt;img alt=\"Page semi-protected\" resource=\"./File:Semi-protection-shackle.svg\" src=\"//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/20px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png\" decoding=\"async\" data-file-width=\"512\" data-file-height=\"512\" data-file-type=\"drawing\" height=\"20\" width=\"20\" srcset=\"//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/30px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x\" class=\"mw-file-element\" data-parsoid=&apos;{\"a\":{\"resource\":\"./File:Semi-protection-shackle.svg\",\"height\":\"20\",\"width\":\"20\"},\"sa\":{\"resource\":\"File:Semi-protection-shackle.svg\"}}&apos;/>&lt;/a>&lt;/span>"}' id="mwCA"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Wikipedia_indefinitely_semi-protected_pages#Banana" about="#mwt5" id="mwCQ"/>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1218072481" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" about="#mwt12" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Infobox botanical product\n","href":"./Template:Infobox_botanical_product"},"params":{"product":{"wt":"Banana"},"image":{"wt":"[[File:Bananavarieties.jpg|250px]]"},"caption":{"wt":"Fruits of four different [[List of banana cultivars|cultivars]]. width: 25em;" about="#mwt12" id="mwDg"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above">Banana</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="./File:Bananavarieties.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img resource="./File:Bananavarieties.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg/250px-Bananavarieties.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="221" data-file-type="bitmap" height="143" width="250" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg/375px-Bananavarieties.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element"/></a></span><div class="infobox-caption">Fruits of four different <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivars</a>. Left to right: plantain, red banana, apple banana, and Cavendish banana</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Source plant(s)</th><td class="infobox-data"><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Part(s) of plant</th><td class="infobox-data">Fruit</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Uses</th><td class="infobox-data">Food</td></tr></tbody></table>

<p id="mwDw">A <b id="mwEA">banana</b> is an elongated, edible <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fruit" title="Fruit" id="mwEQ">fruit</a> – botanically a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)" id="mwEg">berry</a><sup about="#mwt16" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-morton_1-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"morton"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-morton-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwEw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwFA">[1]</span></a></sup> – produced by several kinds of large <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Herbaceous" title="Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" id="mwFQ">herbaceous</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant" id="mwFg">flowering plants</a> in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Genus" title="Genus" id="mwFw">genus</a> <i id="mwGA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)" id="mwGQ">Musa</a></i>. In some countries, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana" id="mwGg">cooking bananas</a> are called plantains, distinguishing them from <b id="mwGw">dessert bananas</b>. The fruit is variable in size, color, and firmness, but is usually elongated and curved, with soft flesh rich in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Starch" title="Starch" id="mwHA">starch</a> covered with a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)" id="mwHQ">rind</a>, which may have a variety of colors when ripe. The fruits grow upward in clusters near the top of the plant. Almost all modern edible seedless (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Parthenocarpy" title="Parthenocarpy" id="mwHg">parthenocarp</a>) bananas come from two wild species – <i id="mwHw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata" id="mwIA">Musa acuminata</a></i> and <i id="mwIQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana" id="mwIg">Musa balbisiana</a></i>. Most cultivated bananas are <i id="mwIw">M. acuminata</i>, <i id="mwJA">M. balbisiana</i>, or hybrids of the two. </p>

<p id="mwJQ"><i id="mwJg">Musa</i> species are native to tropical <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Indomalaya" title="Indomalaya" class="mw-redirect" id="mwJw">Indomalaya</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)" id="mwKA">Australia</a>; they were probably domesticated in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./New_Guinea" title="New Guinea" id="mwKQ">New Guinea</a>. They are grown in 135 countries, primarily for their fruit, and to a lesser extent to make <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Banana_paper" title="Banana paper" id="mwKg">banana paper</a> and textiles, while some are grown as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ornamental_plant" title="Ornamental plant" id="mwKw">ornamental plants</a>. The world's largest producers of bananas in 2022 were <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./India" title="India" id="mwLA">India</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./China" title="China" id="mwLQ">China</a>, which together accounted for approximately 26% of total production.<!--<ref name="FAOSTAT2022"/&#x3E;--> Bananas are eaten raw or cooked in recipes varying from curries to banana chips, fritters, fruit preserves, or simply baked or steamed.</p>

<p id="mwLg">Worldwide, there is no sharp distinction between dessert "bananas" and cooking "plantains": this works well enough in the Americas and Europe, but it breaks down in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia" id="mwLw">Southeast Asia</a> where many more kinds of bananas are grown and eaten. The term "banana" is applied also to other members of the genus <i id="mwMA">Musa</i>, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_coccinea" title="Musa coccinea" id="mwMQ">scarlet banana</a> (<i id="mwMg">Musa coccinea</i>), the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_velutina" title="Musa velutina" id="mwMw">pink banana</a> (<i id="mwNA">Musa velutina</i>), and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fe'i_banana" title="Fe'i banana" id="mwNQ">Fe'i bananas</a>. Members of the genus <i id="mwNg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ensete" title="Ensete" id="mwNw">Ensete</a></i>, such as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ensete_glaucum" title="Ensete glaucum" id="mwOA">snow banana</a> (<i id="mwOQ">Ensete glaucum</i>) and the economically important <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ensete_ventricosum" title="Ensete ventricosum" id="mwOg">false banana</a> (<i id="mwOw">Ensete ventricosum</i>) of Africa are sometimes included. Both genera are in the banana family, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musaceae" title="Musaceae" id="mwPA">Musaceae</a>.</p>

<p id="mwPQ">Banana plantations are subject to damage by parasitic nematodes and insect pests, and to fungal and bacterial diseases, one of the most serious being Panama disease which is caused by a <i id="mwPg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fusarium" title="Fusarium" id="mwPw">Fusarium</a></i> fungus. This and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka" id="mwQA">black sigatoka</a> threaten the production of Cavendish bananas, the main variety eaten in the Western world. Plant breeders are seeking new varieties, but these are difficult to breed given that commercial varieties are seedless. To enable future breeding, banana <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Germplasm" title="Germplasm" id="mwQQ">germplasm</a> is conserved in multiple gene banks around the world.</p>

<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" data-mw='{"autoGenerated":true}' id="mwQg"/></section><section data-mw-section-id="1" id="mwQw"><h2 id="Description">Description</h2>

<p id="mwRA">The banana plant is the largest <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Herbaceous" title="Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" id="mwRQ">herbaceous</a> flowering plant.<sup about="#mwt29" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-PicqINIB00_2-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"PicqINIB00"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-PicqINIB00-2" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 2;" id="mwRg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwRw">[2]</span></a></sup> All the above-ground parts of a banana plant grow from a structure called a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Corm" title="Corm" id="mwSA">corm</a>.<sup about="#mwt20" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17_3-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Stover"},"2":{"wt":"Simmonds"},"3":{"wt":"1987"},"pp":{"wt":"5–17"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" id="mwSQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwSg">[3]</span></a></sup> Plants are normally tall and fairly sturdy with a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tree" title="Tree" id="mwSw">treelike</a> appearance, but what appears to be a trunk is actually a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pseudostem" title="Pseudostem" class="mw-redirect" id="mwTA">pseudostem</a> composed of multiple leaf-stalks (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)" id="mwTQ">petioles</a>). Bananas grow in a wide variety of soils, as long as it is at least <span about="#mwt23" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"60"},"2":{"wt":"cm"},"3":{"wt":"ft"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwTg">60 centimetres (2.0</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt23"> </span><span about="#mwt23">ft)</span> deep, has good drainage and is not compacted.<sup about="#mwt24" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987212_4-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Stover"},"2":{"wt":"Simmonds"},"3":{"wt":"1987"},"p":{"wt":"212"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987212-4" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 4;" id="mwTw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwUA">[4]</span></a></sup> They are among the fastest growing of all plants, with daily surface growth rates recorded from <span about="#mwt27" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"1.4"},"2":{"wt":"m2"},"3":{"wt":"ft2"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwUQ">1.4 square metres (15</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt27"> </span><span about="#mwt27">sq</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt27"> </span><span about="#mwt27">ft)</span> to <span about="#mwt28" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"1.6"},"2":{"wt":"m2"},"3":{"wt":"ft2"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwUg">1.6 square metres (17</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt28"> </span><span about="#mwt28">sq</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt28"> </span><span about="#mwt28">ft)</span>.<sup about="#mwt33" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-5"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-5" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 5;" id="mwUw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwVA">[5]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt37" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-6" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-6"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-6" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 6;" id="mwVQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwVg">[6]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwVw">The leaves of banana plants are composed of a stalk (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)" id="mwWA">petiole</a>) and a blade (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leaf#General_characteristics_of_leaves" title="Leaf" id="mwWQ">lamina</a>). The base of the petiole widens to form a sheath; the tightly packed sheaths make up the pseudostem, which is all that supports the plant. The edges of the sheath meet when it is first produced, making it tubular. As new growth occurs in the centre of the pseudostem, the edges are forced apart.<sup about="#mwt39" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17_3-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Stover"},"2":{"wt":"Simmonds"},"3":{"wt":"1987"},"pp":{"wt":"5–17"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" id="mwWg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwWw">[3]</span></a></sup> Cultivated banana plants vary in height depending on the variety and growing conditions. Most are around <span about="#mwt42" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"5"},"2":{"wt":"m"},"3":{"wt":"ft"},"abbr":{"wt":"on"},"4":{"wt":"0"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwXA">5</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt42"> </span><span about="#mwt42">m (16</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt42"> </span><span about="#mwt42">ft)</span> tall, with a range from 'Dwarf Cavendish' plants at around <span about="#mwt43" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"3"},"2":{"wt":"m"},"3":{"wt":"ft"},"abbr":{"wt":"on"},"4":{"wt":"0"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwXQ">3</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt43"> </span><span about="#mwt43">m (10</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt43"> </span><span about="#mwt43">ft)</span> to 'Gros Michel' at <span about="#mwt44" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"7"},"2":{"wt":"m"},"3":{"wt":"ft"},"abbr":{"wt":"on"},"4":{"wt":"0"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwXg">7</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt44"> </span><span about="#mwt44">m (23</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt44"> </span><span about="#mwt44">ft)</span> or more.<!--{{sfn|Nelson|Ploetz|Kepler|2006|p=26}}--><sup about="#mwt45" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712_7-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Ploetz"},"2":{"wt":"Kepler"},"3":{"wt":"Daniells"},"4":{"wt":"Nelson"},"5":{"wt":"2007"},"p":{"wt":"12"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwXw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwYA">[7]</span></a></sup> Leaves are spirally arranged and may grow <span about="#mwt48" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"2.7"},"2":{"wt":"m"},"3":{"wt":"ft"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwYQ">2.7 metres (8.9</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt48"> </span><span about="#mwt48">ft)</span> long and <span about="#mwt49" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"60"},"2":{"wt":"cm"},"3":{"wt":"ft"},"abbr":{"wt":"on"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwYg">60</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt49"> </span><span about="#mwt49">cm (2.0</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt49"> </span><span about="#mwt49">ft)</span> wide.<sup about="#mwt59" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-morton_1-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"morton"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-morton-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwYw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwZA">[1]</span></a></sup> When a banana plant is mature, the corm stops producing new leaves and begins to form a flower spike or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence" id="mwZQ">inflorescence</a>. A stem develops which grows up inside the pseudostem, carrying the immature inflorescence until eventually it emerges at the top.<sup about="#mwt50" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17_3-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Stover"},"2":{"wt":"Simmonds"},"3":{"wt":"1987"},"pp":{"wt":"5–17"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" id="mwZg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwZw">[3]</span></a></sup> Each pseudostem normally produces a single inflorescence, also known as the "banana heart". After fruiting, the pseudostem dies, but offshoots will normally have developed from the base, so that the plant as a whole is <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Perennial" title="Perennial" id="mwaA">perennial</a>.<sup about="#mwt53" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247_8-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Stover"},"2":{"wt":"Simmonds"},"3":{"wt":"1987"},"pp":{"wt":"244–247"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwaQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwag">[8]</span></a></sup> The inflorescence contains many petal-like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bract" title="Bract" id="mwaw">bracts</a> between rows of flowers. The female flowers (which can develop into fruit) appear in rows further up the stem (closer to the leaves) from the rows of male flowers. The ovary is <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovary_(plants)" title="Ovary (plants)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwbA">inferior</a>, meaning that the tiny petals and other flower parts appear at the tip of the ovary.<sup about="#mwt56" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator2008_9-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Office of the Gene Technology Regulator"},"2":{"wt":"2008"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator2008-9" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 9;" id="mwbQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwbg">[9]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwbw">The banana fruits develop from the banana heart, in a large hanging cluster called a bunch, made up of around 9 tiers called hands, with up to 20 fruits to a hand. A bunch can weigh <span about="#mwt61" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"22"},"2":{"wt":"–"},"3":{"wt":"65"},"4":{"wt":"kg"},"5":{"wt":"lb"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwcA">22–65 kilograms (49–143</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt61"> </span><span about="#mwt61">lb)</span>.<sup about="#mwt65" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-10" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-10"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mwcQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwcg">[10]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwcw">The fruit has been described as a "leathery berry".<sup about="#mwt69" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Smit77_11-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Smit77"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Smit77-11" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 11;" id="mwdA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwdQ">[11]</span></a></sup> There is a protective outer layer (a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)" id="mwdg">peel</a> or skin) with numerous long, thin strings (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vascular_bundle" title="Vascular bundle" id="mwdw">Vascular bundles</a>), which run lengthwise between the skin and the edible inner portion. The inner part of the common yellow dessert variety can be split lengthwise into three sections that correspond to the inner portions of the three <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carpel" title="Carpel" class="mw-redirect" id="mweA">carpels</a> by manually deforming the unopened fruit.<sup about="#mwt70" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Wark04_12-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Wark04"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Wark04-12" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 12;" id="mweQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mweg">[12]</span></a></sup> In cultivated varieties, the seeds are diminished nearly to non-existence; their remnants are tiny black specks inside the fruit.<sup about="#mwt71" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Simm62_13-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Simm62"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Simm62-13" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 13;" id="mwew"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwfA">[13]</span></a></sup></p>

<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" typeof="mw:Extension/gallery" about="#mwt74" data-mw='{"name":"gallery","attrs":{"mode":"packed"},"body":{}}' id="mwfQ">

</section><section data-mw-section-id="2" id="mwqA"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2>

<section data-mw-section-id="3" id="mwqQ"><h3 id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</h3>

<p id="mwqg">A 2011 phylogenomic analysis using nuclear genes indicates the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" id="mwqw">phylogeny</a> of some representatives of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musaceae" title="Musaceae" id="mwrA">Musaceae</a> family. Major edible kinds of banana are shown in <b id="mwrQ">boldface</b>.<sup about="#mwt79" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Christelová_Valárik_Hřibová_De_Langhe_2011_14-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Christelová Valárik Hřibová De Langhe 2011"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Christelová_Valárik_Hřibová_De_Langhe_2011-14"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Christelová_Valárik_Hřibová_De_Langhe_2011-14" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 14;" id="mwrg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwrw">[14]</span></a></sup></p> 

<div class="clade" about="#mwt80" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw="{&quot;parts&quot;:[{&quot;template&quot;:{&quot;target&quot;:{&quot;wt&quot;:&quot;clade\n&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;./Template:Clade&quot;},&quot;params&quot;:{&quot;label1&quot;:{&quot;wt&quot;:&quot;''[[Musaceae]]''&quot;},&quot;1&quot;:{&quot;wt&quot;:&quot;{{clade\n   |label1=''[[Musa (genus)|Musa]]''\n   |1={{clade\n      |label1=Clade I\n      |1={{clade\n         |1={{clade\n            |1={{clade\n               |1={{clade\n                  |1=''[[Musa acuminata#burmannica|Musa acuminata]]'' ssp. <td class="clade-label">Clade<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>II</td>
<td rowspan="2" class="clade-leaf">
<div><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1216293433" about="#mwt99" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles" data-mw='{"name":"templatestyles","attrs":{"src":"Template:Clade/styles.css"},"body":{"extsrc":""}}'/>

<dl id="mwsQ"><dd id="mwsg"><sup id="mwsw">‡</sup> <small id="mwtA">Many <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars" id="mwtQ">cultivated bananas</a> are hybrids of <i id="mwtg">M. acuminata</i> x <i id="mwtw">M. balbisiana</i> (not shown in tree).<sup about="#mwt104" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-WCSP_Mp_15-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"WCSP_Mp"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-WCSP_Mp-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 15;" id="mwuA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwuQ">[15]</span></a></sup></small></dd></dl>

<p id="mwug">Work by Li and colleagues in 2024 identifies three subspecies of <i id="mwuw">M. acuminata</i>, namely sspp. <i id="mwvA">banksii</i>, <i id="mwvQ">malaccensis</i>, and <i id="mwvg">zebrina</i>, as contributing substantially to the <i id="mwvw">Ban</i>, <i id="mwwA">Dh</i>, and <i id="mwwQ">Ze</i> subgenomes of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Triploid" title="Triploid" class="mw-redirect" id="mwwg">triploid</a> cultivated bananas respectively.<sup about="#mwt109" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Li_Yu_Cheng_Chang_2024_16-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Li Yu Cheng Chang 2024"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Li_Yu_Cheng_Chang_2024-16"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Li_Yu_Cheng_Chang_2024-16" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 16;" id="mwww"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwxA">[16]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="4" id="mwxQ"><h3 id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</h3>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt110" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"List of banana cultivars"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwxg"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt110" id="mwxw">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">List of banana cultivars</a></div>

<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwyA"><a href="./File:Nedravazhakola.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwyQ"><img resource="./File:Nedravazhakola.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/220px-Nedravazhakola.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" data-file-type="bitmap" height="165" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/330px-Nedravazhakola.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/440px-Nedravazhakola.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwyg"/></a><figcaption id="mwyw"><i id="mwzA">Musa</i> 'Nendran' <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cultivar" title="Cultivar" id="mwzQ">cultivar</a>, grown widely in the Indian state of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kerala" title="Kerala" id="mwzg">Kerala</a> </figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwzw">The genus <i id="mw0A"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)" id="mw0Q">Musa</a></i> was created by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus" id="mw0g">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1753.<sup about="#mwt118" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-WCSP_Musa_17-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"WCSP_Musa"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-WCSP_Musa-17" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 17;" id="mw0w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw1A">[17]</span></a></sup> The name may be derived from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Antonius_Musa" title="Antonius Musa" id="mw1Q">Antonius Musa</a>, physician to the Emperor <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Augustus" title="Augustus" id="mw1g">Augustus</a>, or Linnaeus may have adapted the Arabic word for banana, <i id="mw1w"><a rel="mw:WikiLink/Interwiki" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/موز" title="wikt:موز" class="extiw" id="mw2A">mauz</a></i>.<sup about="#mwt119" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-HyamPank95_18-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"HyamPank95"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-HyamPank95-18" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 18;" id="mw2Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw2g">[18]</span></a></sup> The ultimate origin of <i id="mw2w">musa</i> may be in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Trans–New_Guinea_languages" title="Trans–New Guinea languages" id="mw3A">Trans–New Guinea languages</a>, which have words similar to "#muku"; from there the name was borrowed into the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages" id="mw3Q">Austronesian languages</a> and across Asia, accompanying the cultivation of the banana as it was brought to new areas, via the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages" id="mw3g">Dravidian languages</a> of India, into Arabic as a <i id="mw3w"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wanderwort" title="Wanderwort" id="mw4A">Wanderwort</a></i>.<sup about="#mwt126" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-19" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-19"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-19" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mw4Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw4g">[19]</span></a></sup> The word "banana" is thought to be of West African origin, possibly from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wolof_language" title="Wolof language" id="mw4w">Wolof</a> word <span title="Wolof-language text" about="#mwt117" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"lang","href":"./Template:Lang"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"wo"},"2":{"wt":"[[wikt:banaana|banaana]]"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mw5A"><i lang="wo"><a rel="mw:WikiLink/Interwiki" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/banaana" title="wikt:banaana" class="extiw">banaana</a></i></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_Wolof-language_text" about="#mwt117" id="mw5Q"/>, and passed into <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./English_language" title="English language" id="mw5g">English</a> via <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Spanish_language" title="Spanish language" id="mw5w">Spanish</a> or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language" id="mw6A">Portuguese</a>.<sup about="#mwt127" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-OEtymD_20-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"OEtymD"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-OEtymD-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 20;" id="mw6Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw6g">[20]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mw6w"><i id="mw7A">Musa</i> is the type genus in the family <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musaceae" title="Musaceae" id="mw7Q">Musaceae</a>. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./APG_III_system" title="APG III system" id="mw7g">APG III system</a> assigns Musaceae to the order <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Zingiberales" title="Zingiberales" id="mw7w">Zingiberales</a>, part of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Commelinid" title="Commelinid" class="mw-redirect" id="mw8A">commelinid</a> clade of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monocotyledon" title="Monocotyledon" id="mw8Q">monocotyledonous</a> flowering plants. Some 70 species of <i id="mw8g">Musa</i> were recognized by the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./World_Checklist_of_Selected_Plant_Families" title="World Checklist of Selected Plant Families" id="mw8w">World Checklist of Selected Plant Families</a> <span about="#mwt130" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"as of","href":"./Template:As_of"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"2013"},"2":{"wt":"January"},"lc":{"wt":"yes"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mw9A">as of January</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt130"> </span><span about="#mwt130">2013</span><sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;" about="#mwt130"><a rel="mw:ExtLink" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Banana&amp;action=edit" class="external text"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span>update<span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></a></sup><span typeof="mw:Nowiki" about="#mwt130"></span><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_containing_potentially_dated_statements_from_January_2013" about="#mwt130"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_containing_potentially_dated_statements" about="#mwt130" id="mw9Q"/>;<sup about="#mwt132" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-WCSP_Musa_17-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"WCSP_Musa"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-WCSP_Musa-17" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 17;" id="mw9g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw9w">[17]</span></a></sup> several produce edible fruit, while others are cultivated as ornamentals.<sup about="#mwt133" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Bail16_21-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Bail16"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Bail16-21" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 21;" id="mw-A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw-Q">[21]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mw-g">The classification of cultivated bananas has long been a problematic issue for taxonomists. Linnaeus originally placed bananas into two species based only on their uses as food: <i id="mw-w">Musa sapientum</i> for dessert bananas and <i id="mw_A">Musa paradisiaca</i> for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Plantain_(true)" title="Plantain (true)" class="mw-redirect" id="mw_Q">plantains</a>. More species names were added, but this approach proved to be inadequate for the number of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cultivar" title="Cultivar" id="mw_g">cultivars</a> in the primary <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Center_of_diversity" title="Center of diversity" class="mw-redirect" id="mw_w">center of diversity</a> of the genus, Southeast Asia. Many of these cultivars were given names that were later discovered to be <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)" id="mwAQA">synonyms</a>.<sup about="#mwt134" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000_22-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Valmayor"},"2":{"wt":"Jamaluddin"},"3":{"wt":"Silayoi"},"4":{"wt":"Kusumo"},"5":{"wt":"2000"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000-22" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 22;" id="mwAQE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQI">[22]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAQM">In a series of papers published from 1947 onwards, Ernest Cheesman showed that Linnaeus's <i id="mwAQQ">Musa sapientum</i> and <i id="mwAQU">Musa paradisiaca</i> were cultivars and descendants of two wild seed-producing species, <i id="mwAQY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata" id="mwAQc">Musa acuminata</a></i> and <i id="mwAQg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana" id="mwAQk">Musa balbisiana</a></i>, both first described by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Luigi_Aloysius_Colla" title="Luigi Aloysius Colla" id="mwAQo">Luigi Aloysius Colla</a>.<sup about="#mwt143" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-DRC_23-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"DRC"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-DRC-23" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 23;" id="mwAQs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQw">[23]</span></a></sup> Cheesman recommended the abolition of Linnaeus's species in favor of reclassifying bananas according to three morphologically distinct groups of cultivars – those primarily exhibiting the botanical characteristics of <i id="mwAQ0">Musa balbisiana</i>, those primarily exhibiting the botanical characteristics of <i id="mwAQ4">Musa acuminata</i>, and those with characteristics of both.<sup about="#mwt140" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000_22-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Valmayor"},"2":{"wt":"Jamaluddin"},"3":{"wt":"Silayoi"},"4":{"wt":"Kusumo"},"5":{"wt":"2000"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000-22" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 22;" id="mwAQ8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwARA">[22]</span></a></sup> Researchers Norman Simmonds and Ken Shepherd proposed a genome-based nomenclature system in 1955. This system eliminated almost all the difficulties and inconsistencies of the earlier classification of bananas based on assigning scientific names to cultivated varieties. Despite this, the original names are still recognized by some authorities, leading to confusion.<sup about="#mwt144" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-DRC_23-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"DRC"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-DRC-23" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 23;" id="mwARE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwARI">[23]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt145" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Porc02_24-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Porc02"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Porc02-24" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mwARM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwARQ">[24]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwARU">The accepted <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Botanical_name" title="Botanical name" id="mwARY">scientific names</a> for most groups of cultivated bananas are <i id="mwARc">Musa acuminata</i> <small id="mwARg">Colla</small> and <i id="mwARk">Musa balbisiana</i> <small id="mwARo">Colla</small> for the ancestral species, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_×_paradisiaca" title="Musa × paradisiaca" id="mwARs"><i id="mwARw">Musa</i> × <i id="mwAR0">paradisiaca</i></a> <small id="mwAR4">L.</small> for the hybrid of the two.<sup about="#mwt147" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-WCSP_Mp_15-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"WCSP_Mp"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-WCSP_Mp-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 15;" id="mwAR8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASA">[15]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="5" id="mwASE"><h3 id="Informal_classification">Informal classification</h3>

<p id="mwASI">In regions such as North America and Europe, <i id="mwASM">Musa</i> fruits offered for sale can be divided into small sweet "bananas" eaten raw when ripe as a dessert, and large starchy "plantains" or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana" id="mwASQ">cooking bananas</a>, which do not have to be ripe. Linnaeus made this distinction when naming two "species" of <i id="mwASU">Musa</i>.<sup about="#mwt148" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002_25-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Valmayor"},"2":{"wt":"Jamaluddin"},"3":{"wt":"Silayoi"},"4":{"wt":"Kusumo"},"5":{"wt":"2000"},"p":{"wt":"2"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002-25" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 25;" id="mwASY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASc">[25]</span></a></sup> Members of the "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Plantain_(true)" title="Plantain (true)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwASg">plantain subgroup</a>" of banana cultivars, most important as food in West Africa and Latin America, correspond to this description, having long pointed fruit. They are described by Ploetz et al. as "true" plantains, distinct from other cooking bananas.<sup about="#mwt151" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200718–19_26-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Ploetz"},"2":{"wt":"Kepler"},"3":{"wt":"Daniells"},"4":{"wt":"Nelson"},"5":{"wt":"2007"},"pp":{"wt":"18–19"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200718–19-26" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 26;" id="mwASk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASo">[26]</span></a></sup></p> 

<p id="mwASs">The cooking bananas of East Africa however belong to a different group, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./East_African_Highland_banana" title="East African Highland banana" class="mw-redirect" id="mwASw">East African Highland bananas</a>.<sup about="#mwt156" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712_7-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Ploetz"},"2":{"wt":"Kepler"},"3":{"wt":"Daniells"},"4":{"wt":"Nelson"},"5":{"wt":"2007"},"p":{"wt":"12"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwAS0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAS4">[7]</span></a></sup> Further, small farmers in Colombia grow a much wider range of cultivars than large commercial plantations do,<sup about="#mwt165" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-GibeDufoGiraSanc09_27-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"GibeDufoGiraSanc09"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-GibeDufoGiraSanc09-27" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 27;" id="mwAS8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATA">[27]</span></a></sup> and in Southeast Asia—the center of diversity for bananas, both wild and cultivated—the distinction between "bananas" and "plantains" does not work. Many bananas are used both raw and cooked. There are starchy cooking bananas which are smaller than those eaten raw. The range of colors, sizes and shapes is far wider than in those grown or sold in Africa, Europe or the Americas.<sup about="#mwt159" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002_25-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Valmayor"},"2":{"wt":"Jamaluddin"},"3":{"wt":"Silayoi"},"4":{"wt":"Kusumo"},"5":{"wt":"2000"},"p":{"wt":"2"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002-25" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 25;" id="mwATE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATI">[25]</span></a></sup> Southeast Asian languages do not make the distinction between "bananas" and "plantains" that is made in English. Thus both Cavendish dessert bananas and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Saba_banana" title="Saba banana" id="mwATM">Saba cooking bananas</a> are called <i id="mwATQ">pisang</i> in Malaysia and Indonesia, <i id="mwATU">kluai</i> in Thailand and <i id="mwATY">chuối</i> in Vietnam.<sup about="#mwt162" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20008–12_28-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Valmayor"},"2":{"wt":"Jamaluddin"},"3":{"wt":"Silayoi"},"4":{"wt":"Kusumo"},"5":{"wt":"2000"},"pp":{"wt":"8–12"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20008–12-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 28;" id="mwATc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATg">[28]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fe'i_banana" title="Fe'i banana" id="mwATk">Fe'i bananas</a>, grown and eaten in the islands of the Pacific, are derived from a different wild species. Most Fe'i bananas are cooked, but <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Karat_banana" title="Karat banana" id="mwATo">Karat bananas</a>, which are short and squat with bright red skins, are eaten raw.<sup about="#mwt166" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Engl03_29-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Engl03"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Engl03-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwATs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATw">[29]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="6" id="mwAT0"><h2 id="History">History</h2>

<section data-mw-section-id="7" id="mwAT4"><h3 id="Domestication">Domestication</h3>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt167" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"See also","href":"./Template:See_also"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Musa acuminata"},"2":{"wt":"Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia"},"3":{"wt":"East African Highland bananas"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAT8"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt167" id="mwAUA">See also: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Domesticated_plants_and_animals_of_Austronesia" title="Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia">Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./East_African_Highland_bananas" title="East African Highland bananas" class="mw-redirect">East African Highland bananas</a></div>

<p id="mwAUE">The earliest domestication of bananas (<i id="mwAUI"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)" id="mwAUM">Musa</a></i> spp.) was from naturally occurring <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Parthenocarpic" title="Parthenocarpic" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUQ">parthenocarpic</a> (seedless) individuals of <i id="mwAUU"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_banksii" title="Musa banksii" id="mwAUY">Musa banksii</a></i> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./New_Guinea" title="New Guinea" id="mwAUc">New Guinea</a>.<!--<ref name=apscience/&#x3E;--><sup about="#mwt177" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelsonPloetzKepler2006_30-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Nelson"},"2":{"wt":"Ploetz"},"3":{"wt":"Kepler"},"4":{"wt":"2006"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelsonPloetzKepler2006-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 30;" id="mwAUg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAUk">[30]</span></a></sup> These were cultivated by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Papuans" title="Papuans" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUo">Papuans</a> before the arrival of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples" id="mwAUs">Austronesian-speakers</a>. Numerous <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phytolith" title="Phytolith" id="mwAUw">phytoliths</a> of bananas have been recovered from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kuk_Swamp" title="Kuk Swamp" id="mwAU0">Kuk Swamp</a> archaeological site and dated to around 10,000 to 6,500 <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Before_Present" title="Before Present" id="mwAU4">BP</a>.<sup about="#mwt180" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denham2011"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Denham2011-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAU8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAVA">[31]</span></a></sup><!--<ref name="Perrier2009"/&#x3E;--><sup about="#mwt181" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller2015_32-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Fuller2015"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Fuller2015-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwAVE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAVI">[32]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Foraging" title="Foraging" id="mwAVM">Foraging</a> humans in this area began domestication in the late <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene" id="mwAVQ">Pleistocene</a> using <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Transplanting" title="Transplanting" id="mwAVU">transplantation</a> and early <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tillage" title="Tillage" id="mwAVY">cultivation</a> methods.<sup about="#mwt185" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts-et-al-2017_33-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Roberts-et-al-2017"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-33"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-33" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwAVc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAVg">[33]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt189" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Harris-Hillman-1989_34-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Harris-Hillman-1989"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-34"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 34;" id="mwAVk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAVo">[34]</span></a></sup> By the early to middle of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Holocene" title="Holocene" id="mwAVs">Holocene</a> the process was complete.<sup about="#mwt190" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Harris-Hillman-1989_34-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Harris-Hillman-1989"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 34;" id="mwAVw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAV0">[34]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt191" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts-et-al-2017_33-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Roberts-et-al-2017"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-33" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwAV4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAV8">[33]</span></a></sup> From New Guinea, cultivated bananas spread westward into <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Island_Southeast_Asia" title="Island Southeast Asia" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAWA">Island Southeast Asia</a> through proximity (not migrations). They <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)" id="mwAWE">hybridized</a> with other (possibly independently domesticated) <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Subspecies" title="Subspecies" id="mwAWI">subspecies</a> of <i id="mwAWM">Musa acuminata</i> as well as <i id="mwAWQ">M. balbisiana</i> in the Philippines, northern New Guinea, and possibly <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Halmahera" title="Halmahera" id="mwAWU">Halmahera</a>. These hybridization events produced the triploid <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars" id="mwAWY">cultivars of bananas</a> commonly grown today.<sup about="#mwt192" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denham2011"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Denham2011-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAWc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAWg">[31]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="8" id="mwAWk"><h3 id="Spread">Spread</h3>

<p id="mwAWo">From Island Southeast Asia, bananas became part of the staple domesticated crops of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples" id="mwAWs">Austronesian peoples</a> and were spread during their voyages and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Austronesian_maritime_trade_network" title="Austronesian maritime trade network" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAWw">ancient maritime trading routes</a> into Oceania, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./East_Africa" title="East Africa" id="mwAW0">East Africa</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./South_Asia" title="South Asia" id="mwAW4">South Asia</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Indochina" title="Indochina" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAW8">Indochina</a>.<sup about="#mwt198" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denham2011"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Denham2011-31"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Denham2011-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAXA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXE">[31]</span></a></sup><!--<ref name="Perrier2009"/&#x3E;--><sup about="#mwt202" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller2015_32-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Fuller2015"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Fuller2015-32"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Fuller2015-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwAXI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXM">[32]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAXQ">These ancient introductions resulted in the banana subgroup now known as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./True_plantains" title="True plantains" id="mwAXU">true plantains</a>, which include the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./East_African_Highland_bananas" title="East African Highland bananas" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXY">East African Highland bananas</a> and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pacific_plantains" title="Pacific plantains" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXc">Pacific plantains</a> (the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Iholena_bananas" title="Iholena bananas" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXg">Iholena</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Maoli-Popo'ulu_bananas" title="Maoli-Popo'ulu bananas" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAXk">Maoli-Popo'ulu</a> subgroups). East African Highland bananas originated from banana populations introduced to Madagascar probably from the region between <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Java" title="Java" id="mwAXo">Java</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Borneo" title="Borneo" id="mwAXs">Borneo</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./New_Guinea" title="New Guinea" id="mwAXw">New Guinea</a>; while Pacific plantains were introduced to the Pacific Islands from either eastern New Guinea or the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago" id="mwAX0">Bismarck Archipelago</a>.<sup about="#mwt204" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denham2011"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Denham2011-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAX4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAX8">[31]</span></a></sup></p><!--<ref name="Perrier2009"/&#x3E;-->

<p id="mwAYA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phytolith" title="Phytolith" id="mwAYE">Phytolith</a> discoveries in Cameroon dating to the first millennium BCE<sup about="#mwt210" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Mbid00_35-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Mbid00"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Mbid00-35" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 35;" id="mwAYI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYM">[35]</span></a></sup> triggered an as yet unresolved debate about the date of first cultivation in Africa. There is linguistic evidence that bananas were known in East Africa or Madagascar around that time.<sup about="#mwt211" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Zell05_36-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Zell05"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Zell05-36" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mwAYQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYU">[36]</span></a></sup><!--"Linguistische Studien deuten darauf hin, dass die Banane in Ostafrika (Madagaskar) bereits vor 3.000 Jahren Fuß gefasst (DE LANGHE et al., 1994; DE LANGHE and DE MARET, 1999) und sich dann über den Kontinent bis nach Westafrika ausgebreitet hat." &#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D; citing De Langhe E.A., R. Swennen &#x26; D. Vuylsteke, 1994&#x2D;5. Plantain in the early Bantu world. Azania 29&#x2D;30: 147&#x2D;60. &#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D; and &#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D;&#x2D; De Langhe E.A. &#x26; P. de Maret. 1999. Tracking the banana: its significance in early agriculture. In ‘The Prehistory of Food’ (Chris Gosden and John Hather, eds). Routledge, London and New York.--> The earliest prior evidence indicates that cultivation dates to no earlier than the late 6th century CE.<sup about="#mwt212" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Lejj05_37-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Lejj05"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Lejj05-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 37;" id="mwAYY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYc">[37]</span></a></sup> It is likely, however, that bananas were brought at least to Madagascar if not to the East African coast during the phase of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Malagasy_people" title="Malagasy people" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAYg">Malagasy</a> colonization of the island from South East Asia c. 400 CE.<sup about="#mwt213" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Rand09_38-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Rand09"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Rand09-38" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAYk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYo">[38]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Glucanase" title="Glucanase" id="mwAYs">Glucanase</a> and two other proteins specific to bananas were found in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dental_calculus" title="Dental calculus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAYw">dental calculus</a> from the early <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Iron_Age" title="Iron Age" id="mwAY0">Iron Age</a> (12th century BCE) <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Philistines" title="Philistines" id="mwAY4">Philistines</a> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tel_Erani" title="Tel Erani" id="mwAY8">Tel Erani</a> in the southern <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Levant" title="Levant" id="mwAZA">Levant</a>.<sup about="#mwt217" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-39" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-39"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 39;" id="mwAZE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZI">[39]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAZM">Another wave of introductions later spread bananas to other parts of tropical Asia, particularly Indochina and the Indian subcontinent.<sup about="#mwt223" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Denham2011"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Denham2011-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAZQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZU">[31]</span></a></sup><!--<ref name="Perrier2009"&#x3E;{{cite journal|display&#x2D;authors=3 |last1=Perrier |first1=Xavier |last2=Bakry |first2=Frédéric |last3=Carreel |first3=Françoise |last4=Jenny |first4=Christophe |last5=Horry |first5=Jean&#x2D;Pierre |last6=Lebot |first6=Vincent |last7=Hippolyte |first7=Isabelle |title=Combining Biological Approaches to Shed Light on the Evolution of Edible Bananas |journal=Ethnobotany Research &#x26; Applications |date=2009 |volume=7 |pages=199–216 |url=http://journals.sfu.ca/era/index.php/era/article/download/362/231 |doi=10.17348/era.7.0.199&#x2D;216 |hdl=10125/12515 |access&#x2D;date=October 27, 2019 |archive&#x2D;date=November 16, 2019 |archive&#x2D;url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191116055142/http://journals.sfu.ca/era/index.php/era/article/download/362/231 |url&#x2D;status=live |doi&#x2D;access=free |hdl&#x2D;access=free }}</ref&#x3E;--> However, there is evidence that bananas were known to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAZY">Indus Valley civilisation</a> from phytoliths recovered from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kot_Diji" title="Kot Diji" id="mwAZc">Kot Diji</a> archaeological site in Pakistan. This may indicate very early dispersal of bananas by Austronesian traders by sea from as early as 2000 BCE, or they may have come from local wild <i id="mwAZg">Musa</i> species used for fiber or as ornamentals, not food.<sup about="#mwt224" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller2015_32-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Fuller2015"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Fuller2015-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwAZk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZo">[32]</span></a></sup> Southeast Asia remains the region of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Center_of_diversity" title="Center of diversity" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAZs">primary diversity</a> of the banana. Areas of secondary diversity are found in Africa, indicating a long history of banana cultivation there.<sup about="#mwt220" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson20077_40-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Ploetz"},"2":{"wt":"Kepler"},"3":{"wt":"Daniells"},"4":{"wt":"Nelson"},"5":{"wt":"2007"},"p":{"wt":"7"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson20077-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAZw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAZ0">[40]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="9" id="mwAZ4"><h3 id="Arab_Agricultural_Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</h3>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt225" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"see","href":"./Template:See"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Arab Agricultural Revolution"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAZ8"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt225" id="mwAaA">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a></div>

<p id="mwAaE">The banana may have been present in isolated locations elsewhere in the Middle East on the eve of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Islam" title="Islam" id="mwAaI">Islam</a>. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam" id="mwAaM">spread of Islam</a> was followed by far-reaching diffusion. There are numerous references to it in Islamic texts (such as poems and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hadith" title="Hadith" id="mwAaQ">hadiths</a>) beginning in the 9th century. By the 10th century the banana appears in texts from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)" id="mwAaU">Palestine</a> and Egypt. From there it diffused into North Africa and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus" id="mwAaY">Muslim Iberia</a> during the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution" id="mwAac">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>.<sup about="#mwt236" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_1974_41-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Watson 1974"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Watson_1974-41"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Watson_1974-41" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 41;" id="mwAag"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAak">[41]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt237" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Wats83_42-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Wats83"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Wats83-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mwAao"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAas">[42]</span></a></sup> An article on banana tree cultivation is included in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ibn_al-'Awwam" title="Ibn al-'Awwam" id="mwAaw">Ibn al-'Awwam</a>'s 12th-century agricultural work, <i id="mwAa0">Kitāb al-Filāḥa</i> (<i id="mwAa4">Book on Agriculture</i>).<sup about="#mwt241" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-43" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-43"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-43" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mwAa8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbA">[43]</span></a></sup> During the Middle Ages, bananas from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Granada" title="Granada" id="mwAbE">Granada</a> were considered among the best in the Arab world.<sup about="#mwt242" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Wats83_42-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Wats83"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Wats83-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mwAbI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbM">[42]</span></a></sup> Bananas were certainly grown in the Christian <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus" id="mwAbQ">Kingdom of Cyprus</a> by the late medieval period. Writing in 1458, the Italian traveller and writer <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gabriele_Capodilista" title="Gabriele Capodilista" id="mwAbU">Gabriele Capodilista</a> wrote favourably of the extensive farm produce of the estates at Episkopi, near modern-day <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Limassol" title="Limassol" id="mwAbY">Limassol</a>, including the region's banana plantations.<sup about="#mwt243" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Jenn92_44-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Jenn92"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Jenn92-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 44;" id="mwAbc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbg">[44]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="10" id="mwAbk"><h3 id="Columbian_exchange">Columbian exchange</h3>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt244" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"see","href":"./Template:See"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Columbian exchange"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAbo"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt244" id="mwAbs">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div>

<p id="mwAbw">Bananas were encountered by European explorers during the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Magellan_expedition" title="Magellan expedition" id="mwAb0">Magellan expedition</a> in 1521, in both <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Guam" title="Guam" id="mwAb4">Guam</a> and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Philippines" title="Philippines" id="mwAb8">Philippines</a>. Lacking a name for the fruit, the ship's historian <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Antonio_Pigafetta" title="Antonio Pigafetta" id="mwAcA">Antonio Pigafetta</a> described them as "figs more than one <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Palm_(unit)" title="Palm (unit)" id="mwAcE">palm</a> long."<sup about="#mwt256" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-45" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-45"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-45" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 45;" id="mwAcI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcM">[45]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt260" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Nowell_46-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Nowell"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Nowell-46"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Nowell-46" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 46;" id="mwAcQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcU">[46]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap" about="#mwt252" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"rp","href":"./Template:Rp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"130, 132"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAcY"><span title="Page / location: 130, 132"><span typeof="mw:Entity">:</span><span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>130,<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>132<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span></span></sup> Bananas were introduced to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./South_America" title="South America" id="mwAcc">South America</a> by Portuguese sailors who brought them from West Africa in the 16th century.<sup about="#mwt261" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Botgard_47-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Botgard"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Botgard-47" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 47;" id="mwAcg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAck">[47]</span></a></sup> Southeast Asian banana cultivars, as well as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Abaca" title="Abaca" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAco">abaca</a> grown for fibers, were introduced to North and Central America by the Spanish from the Philippines, via the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Manila_galleons" title="Manila galleons" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAcs">Manila galleons</a>.<sup about="#mwt265" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-48" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-48"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-48" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 48;" id="mwAcw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAc0">[48]</span></a></sup> Many <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Musa_(genus)#Species" title="Musa (genus)" id="mwAc4">wild banana species</a> and cultivars exist in India, China, and Southeast Asia.<sup about="#mwt266" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Peed11_49-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Peed11"}}'><a href="./Banana#cite_note-Peed11-49" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 49;" id="mwAc8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdA">[49]</span></a></sup></p>

<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines center" typeof="mw:Extension/gallery" about="#mwt278" data-mw='{"name":"gallery","attrs":{"class":"center","mode":"nolines","widths":"250px","heights":"180px"},"body":{}}' id="mwAdE">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px;" id="mwAdI"><div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;" id="mwAdM"><span typeof="mw:File" id="mwAdQ"><a href="./File:Banana_ancestors_(Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana)_original_range.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Original native ranges of the ancestors of modern edible bananas.
